A) They were afraid of the sword.
B) They were unable to do so.
C) They did not believe in them.
D) They were not asked.

Key Takeaway
The silence of opponents in the face of miracles is proof of their inability to replicate them, thus confirming prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter undeniable evidence of truth in your own life—like a clear sign from Allah—do you respond with immediate obedience, or do you find ways to rationalize your inaction? What would it take for you to admit your inability to resist and submit fully?
?
The sorcerers and physicians recognized that opposing the prophets would only confirm their truth. Are there areas in your life where you resist the truth because acknowledging it would require changing your behavior? What is holding you back from surrendering to what you know is right?
?
If you were in the position of those sorcerers, would you have had the humility to admit your inability? Or would pride have driven you to futile opposition? Ask yourself today: in what ways are you still trying to 'oppose' God's signs through disobedience or neglect? What will it take for you to stop?

The Sword as a Deterrent from Opposing the Quran
Baqillani refutes the claim that the sword deterred opponents from producing a similar Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sword argument does not undermine the Quran's miraculous nature and how Baqillani refutes it.

Core Idea
The sword was not the deterrent; inability was.

Baqillani addresses a common objection: maybe the Quran's opponents didn't produce something similar because they feared the sword, not because they couldn't. He argues that their continued production of poetry and oratory during war shows fear wasn't the barrier. In this lesson, we examine his reasoning and see why the sword argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a rebuttal.
The objector claims the sword was the deterrent, not inability.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the exact words where the objector states that belief in the sword's effectiveness was the deterrent.
Look for 'اعتقادهم أن السيف أنجع'.
3
See how Baqillani refutes it
Notice that Baqillani points out that the opponents continued producing poetry and oratory even during war. If they could produce speech like the Quran, they would have done so then.
The key phrase: 'كما أنهم كانوا يأتون مع ذلك بالشعر والرجز والخطابة والرسائل'.
4
Understand the logical necessity
Reflect on why their continued production of other speech proves inability, not fear. The sword did not stop them from speaking, so why would it stop them from imitating the Quran?
The conclusion: 'وفي ترك ذلك دليل على بطلان ما قلتم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون الصارف للقوم عن معارضته اعتقادهم أن السيف أنجع في أمره وأحسم لمادة شبهته لا للعجز ذلك؟ قيل له: لو كان في قدرة القوم التكلم بمثل القرآن لأتوا به مع نصب الحرب كما أنهم كانوا يأتون مع ذلك بالشعر والرجز والخطابة والرسائل وكل ما هو من طباعهم وفي ترك ذلك دليل على بطلان ما قلتم.

It is said to him: If the people were capable of speaking with something like the Quran, they would have come with it amidst waging war, just as they used to come with poetry, rajaz, oratory, letters, and everything of their natural disposition; and in their abandoning that is evidence of the falsity of what you say.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the miracle of the Quran. The objector suggests that the opponents did not produce a similar Quran because they feared the sword (i.e., military action) from the prophet, not because they were unable. The answer is that if they had the ability to produce speech like the Quran, they would have done so even during warfare, just as they continued to produce poetry and orations. Their failure to produce anything comparable, despite their natural linguistic ability, proves that the deterrent was inability, not fear. The conclusion is that the objector's claim is false.

Misconception: The opponents of the Prophet did not produce a similar Quran because they feared the sword (military action).
Correction: The sword did not deter them, as they continued to produce poetry and oratory during war. Their failure was due to inability, not fear.

Checkpoint

A) Inability.
B) Fear of the sword.
C) Lack of interest.
D) Following religion.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' ability to produce poetry and oratory during war proves their failure to match the Quran was due to inability, not fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The opponents continued to produce eloquent speech in other forms but failed when it came to matching the Quran. In what ways do you excel in worldly matters yet fail to produce the spiritual excellence the Quran demands? What is holding you back from that transformation?

Refuting the Excuse of Fear of Doubt
Baqillani refutes the claim that fear of doubt prevented opponents from producing a similar Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that fear of doubt cannot be a valid excuse for not producing a similar Quran.

Core Idea
Since the Quran is in their own language, they are the best judges of its inimitability.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to a hypothetical objection: that the Arabs did not challenge the Quran because they feared creating doubt among the Prophet's followers. He dismantles this excuse by appealing to their linguistic expertise and highlighting the stronger motivation of hope to expose falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
First, read the objection that the opponents might raise: fear of doubt prevented them from opposing the Quran. Notice how Baqillani presents it as a hypothetical 'if they say'.
2
Analyze the First Refutation
Baqillani's first response is that the language is their own, so they cannot claim doubt about what is similar to their speech. Identify the key phrases: 'اللسان لسانهم واللغة لغتهم'.
This refutation relies on their native linguistic ability.
3
Examine the Second Refutation
The second point is that hope (رجاء) of proving the Quran false should outweigh any fear. Ask yourself: why would hope be a stronger motivator?
The logic: if they could easily expose falsehood, they would have done so despite any fear.
4
Consider the Incomplete Ending
The passage ends abruptly with a reference to the people of Moses. Note that the argument is incomplete here, but the core refutation is clear.
This may be continued in later portions of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون المانع لهم من معارضته هو خوفهم من دخول الشبهة على أوليائه وقوله لهم: إنه ليس بعروض لما أتيت به؟ قيل لهم: هذا باطل لأن اللسان لسانهم واللغة لغتهم وهي طباع لهم ولا شبهة عليهم في معرفة ما هو بوزن كلامهم ولا مجال ولا مسرح للشك في هذا الباب ثم يقال لهم: فبإزاء الخوف من ذلك الرجاء لوضوح بطلان ما أتى به لهم ووقوفهم عليه فكيف لم يبعثهم هذا على معارضته؟ ويقال لهم في هذه المسألة والتي قبلها: ما أنكرتم أن يكون هذا هو الصارف لقوم موسى
If they say: What do you deny that the preventer for them from opposing him is their fear of doubt entering upon his supporters and his saying to them: It is not a display of what I have brought? It is said to them: This is false because the tongue is their tongue and the language is their language, and they are natural to them, and there is no doubt upon them in knowing what is of the weight of their speech, and there is no field or arena for doubt in this matter. Then it is said to them: In exchange for that fear, there is hope due to the clarity of the falsity of what he brought to them and their awareness of it, so how did this not impel them to oppose him? And it is said to them in this issue and the one before it: What do you deny that this is the deterrent for the people of Moses...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is another objection and answer. The objectors claim that the opponents did not produce a similar Quran because they feared that doing so would create doubt among the prophet's followers, and because the prophet said it is not a display of something he brought. The answer argues that since the Quran is in their own language, they are the best judges of whether it is similar to their speech. There is no room for doubt. Moreover, the hope of exposing the prophet's falsehood would outweigh any fear. The final sentence ties this argument to the previous issue and begins to refer to the people of Moses, but the argument is incomplete in this window.

Misconception: Fear of doubt is a valid excuse for not opposing the Quran.
Correction: Baqillani shows it is invalid because the Arabs were native speakers and could easily judge, and hope of refutation would have overcome fear.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what makes the claim of fear of doubt false?
A) The Quran is not in their language.
B) The language is their own, so they can judge.
C) They were afraid of the sword.

Key Takeaway
Fear of doubt does not excuse the Arabs from opposing the Quran because they are native speakers and had stronger motives to expose falsehood.

?
Baqillani points out that hope of exposing falsehood should have motivated the Arabs to confront the Quran. What hopes or fears are preventing you from confronting the truth of the Quran in your own life? Will you let hope of truth outweigh your fears?

The Sword Objection: Why Didn't They Imitate the Quran?
Baqillani refutes the objection that fear of the sword prevented opposition to the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the claim that fear of the sword, rather than inability, prevented the Arabs from meeting the Quranic challenge.

Core Idea
Fear of the sword didn't stop them; they still produced poetry in war.

Imam Baqillani addresses a critical objection against the miraculous nature of the Quran. Some argued that the Arabs refrained from opposing the Quran not because they couldn't, but because they feared the Prophet's military power. In this passage, Baqillani dismantles this objection by pointing to the Arabs' continued literary output even during wartime.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the structure of the objection and answer.
Notice the concise style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that the polytheists were deterred by the threat of the sword, not by their inability to imitate the Quran.
This is a common challenge against the miracle of the Quran.
3
Analyze the refutation
Baqillani counters by noting that the Arabs continued to produce poetry, war songs, and speeches even during war—so if they could imitate the Quran, they would have done so then.
Their failure proves inability.
4
Reflect on the proof
Consider why this argument is effective: it uses observable historical behavior to disprove the objection.
This is a form of argument from absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في المعارضة والسيف فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون الصارف للقوم عن معارضته اعتقادهم أن السيفَ أنْجَعُ في أمره وأحسم لمادة شُبهته لا للعجز ذلك؟ قيل له: لو كان في قدرة القوم التكلم بمثل القرآن لأتوا به مع نَصْبِ الحرب كما أنهم كانوا يأتون مع ذلك بالشعر والرّجز والخطابة والرسائل وكل ما هو من طباعهم وفي ترك ذلك دليل على بطلان ما قلتم.
Issue concerning opposition and the sword. If someone says: What prevents you [from accepting] that what turned the people away from opposing it [the Quran] was their belief that the sword was more effective in his matter and more decisive in cutting off the substance of his doubt, not inability? It is said to him: If the people had the ability to speak like the Quran, they would have brought it along with the waging of war, just as they brought along with that poetry, war songs, oratory, epistles, and everything that is of their natural disposition. And in their abandoning that is evidence of the falsehood of what you say.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer (مسألة) concerning the reason why the polytheists did not meet the challenge of the Quran. The objector argues that their abstention was not due to inability but due to fear of the sword and the political consequences of admitting Muhammad's truth. Baqillani refutes this by appealing to historical fact: the Arabs were actively engaged in warfare and still produced poetry, war songs, speeches, and letters—all of which are part of their natural linguistic disposition. If they had been capable of producing something like the Quran, they would have done so in the context of war to refute the Prophet. Their failure to do so proves that their abstention was due to inability, not fear.

Key Terms
المعارضة
السيف
الصارف
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that inability prevented imitation is circular and unprovable.
Correction: Baqillani's argument is based on observable historical behavior (poetry during war), not circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use to refute the claim that fear of the sword prevented opposition to the Quran?
A) The fact that the Arabs were cowards.
B) The fact that the Arabs continued to produce poetry and oratory during war.
C) The fact that the Quran is incomparably eloquent.
D) The fact that the Prophet was protected by God.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the Arabs' continued production of poetry during wartime proves that their failure to imitate the Quran was due to inability, not fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a truth that challenges your comfort, do you find yourself searching for excuses (like fear of consequences) to avoid responding, or do you honestly confront your own inability to accept it? What does your reaction reveal about your submission to the truth?
?
The Arabs produced poetry during war, but not the Quran. In your life, what activities do you continue even under pressure, and what do you avoid that might expose your limitations? Are you hiding behind fear when the real issue is your reluctance to admit your weakness?
?
If you were in the place of the polytheists, would you have been among those who made excuses, or would you have recognized the clear evidence of inability and submitted? What would it take for you to submit to the Quran's challenge today?

Objection and Answer on Opposition and Doubt
Baqillani refutes the objection that fear of causing doubt prevented opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that fear of causing doubt prevented the Arabs from opposing the Quran, using linguistic expertise and logical consistency.

Core Idea
Native speakers cannot doubt their own language; thus fear of doubt is no excuse.

The objector tries to excuse the polytheists' failure to produce a challenge to the Quran by claiming they feared sowing doubt among believers. Baqillani dismantles this excuse by pointing to their native linguistic mastery and the stronger motive of disproving falsehood. He then turns the argument back on the objector, showing it applies equally to other prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let Baqillani's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the objector's claim and Baqillani's refutations
Find the objection: fear of doubt and the Prophet's statement. Then locate Baqillani's two refutations: linguistic expertise and hope outweighing fear.
Notice how he uses logical reasoning rather than appeal to authority.
3
Examine the turning of the argument
See how Baqillani applies the same logic to previous prophets (Moses and Jesus), forcing the objector into inconsistency.
This is a classic refutation by analogy.

مسألة في المعارضة والشبهة فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون المانع لهم من معارضته هو خوفهم من دخول الشُّبهة على أوليائه وقوله لهم: إنه ليس بعروض لما أتيتُ به؟ قيل لهم: هذا باطل لأن اللسان لسانهم واللغة لغتهم وهي طباع لهم ولا شبهة عليهم في معرفة ما هو بوزن كلامهم ولا مجال ولا مسرح للشك في هذا الباب ثم يقال لهم: فبإزاء الخوف من ذلك الرجاء لوضوح بطلان ما أتى به لهم ووقوفهم عليه فكيف لم يبعثهم هذا على معارضته؟ ويقال لهم في هذه المسألة والتي قبلها: ما أنكرتم أن يكون هذا هو الصارف لقوم موسى وعيسى عن معارضة ما ادعوه آيةً لهما؟ فكل شيء أجابونا به فهو جوابنا.
Issue concerning opposition and doubt. If they say: What prevents you [from accepting] that what prevented them from opposing him was their fear of doubt entering upon his followers and his saying to them: 'It is not a display/argument for what I have brought'? It is said to them: This is false, because the tongue is their tongue, and the language is their language, and it is natural to them, and there is no doubt upon them in knowing what is of the weight of their speech, and there is no room or open space for doubt in this matter. Then it is said to them: Against the fear of that is the hope due to the clarity of the falsehood of what he brought to them and their knowledge of it; so why did this not impel them to oppose it? And it is said to them in this issue and the one before it: What prevents you that this is what turned the people of Moses and Jesus away from opposing what they claimed as a sign for them? For whatever they answer us with, it is our answer.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer (مسألة). The objector claims that the polytheists refrained from opposing the Quran because they feared that their attempt might cause doubt among the Prophet's followers, and also because the Prophet told them that their opposition would not be a valid challenge. Baqillani refutes this by noting that the Arabs were native speakers of Arabic; they were experts in their own language and could easily recognize whether something matched the style of their speech. Therefore, no doubt could arise. Furthermore, he points out that the hope of exposing the Quran's supposed falsehood should have outweighed the fear of causing doubt. If they truly believed the Quran was false, they would have been motivated to refute it. Finally, Baqillani turns the argument back on the objector: if they accept that fear of doubt prevented opposition to the Quran, they must also accept that the same fear prevented the people of Moses and Jesus from opposing their miracles. Thus, the objector's own logic leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
الشبهة
المانع
اللسان
الوزن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubt could affect even expert native speakers when it comes to assessing a challenge.
Correction: Baqillani asserts that native speakers have no doubt about the style of their own language; they can judge with certainty.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say that the opponents could not have been prevented by fear of doubt?
A) Because the Prophet prevented them from trying.
B) Because they were not native Arabic speakers.
C) Because they were experts in Arabic and could judge with certainty.
D) Because they were afraid of the sword.

Key Takeaway
Native linguistic expertise and logical motivation disprove the excuse of fear of doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The polytheists hoped that the Quran would be proven false, but they did not act on that hope. What falsehood in your own life are you passively hoping will be exposed without you taking a stand?
?
This lesson argues that the fear of causing doubt is no excuse when truth is clear. Where in your life are you prioritizing harmony over truth, and what is stopping you from opposing what is false even when you know it is wrong?

Why Partial Ability Does Not Imply Full Ability
Baqillani argues that inability to produce a whole does not imply inability to produce parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the Quran's miraculousness lies in its whole composition, and that human ability to produce individual words does not entail ability to produce the entire Quran.

Core Idea
Partial ability does not imply total ability — the whole can be beyond human capacity.

Baqillani addresses a common objection to the Quran's miraculousness: if humans can produce words like the Quran, why can't they produce the whole Quran? He uses sharp analogies to show that small capacity does not grant large capacity. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding i'jaz (miraculousness).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Baqillani sets up the opponent's claim and then responds with 'يقال له' (it is said to him).
The opponent argues: if people can produce a word or two, they should be able to produce the whole Quran.
2
Identify the analogies
Baqillani gives three analogies: drinking a sip vs. the ocean, lifting a small weight vs. mountains, and cutting a short distance vs. ascending to the sky. Notice how each shows that ability to do a little does not mean ability to do a lot.
3
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the Quran's composition is a sign (miracle) for Muhammad, even if less than a sura is not miraculous for others. The whole is what matters.
This refutes the objection by showing that the inability to produce the whole does not follow from ability to produce parts.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this argument protects the concept of i'jaz. It shows that the Quran's miraculousness is not diminished by human ability to produce similar individual words or phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في صفة الإعجاز في القرآن فإن قال قائل: فإذا قَدَرَ العباد عندكم على مثل الكلمة والكلمتين والحرف والحرفين فما أنكرتم أن يقْدِرُوا على مثل جميعه وألا يكون في ذلك إعجاز؟ يقال له: لو وجب ما قلته لَوَجَبَ إذا قدر الناس على مثل ما سألت عنه أن يكونوا كلهم شعراء وخطباء وأصحاب نظم ورسائل لقدرتهم على الكلمة والاثنتين وهذا جهل ممن صار إليه وليس يجب، إذا تعذر على الإنسان نظم الكثير، أن يتعذر عليه نظم اليسير؛ كما لا يجب إذا تعذر عليه شرب ماء البحار والأنهار أن يتعذر عليه شرب الجُرعة والجرعتين، وإذا تعذر عليه الصعود إلى السماء وحمل الجبال أن يتعذر عليه قطع الذراع إلى فوق والذراعين وحَمْلُ الرِّطْل والرطلين وإذا كان حمل الجبال والصعود إلى السماء آية لمن ظهر على يده وجب أن يكون نظم القرآن آية لمن أتى به وإن لم يكن نظم ما دون سورة منه آية لأحد.
Issue concerning the description of miraculousness in the Quran. If someone says: Since according to you the servants are able [to produce] something like a word or two words or a letter or two letters, what prevents you from [accepting] that they are able [to produce] something like all of it, and that there is no miraculousness in that? It is said to him: If what you said were necessary, then if people were able [to produce] what you asked about, it would be necessary that all of them be poets, orators, masters of verse, and epistles because of their ability [to produce] a word or two. And this is ignorance on the part of whoever holds it. And it is not necessary that if it is impossible for a person to compose much, that it be impossible for him to compose a little; just as it is not necessary that if it is impossible for him to drink the water of the seas and rivers, that it be impossible for him to drink a sip or two sips. And if it is impossible for him to ascend to the sky and carry mountains, it is not necessary that it be impossible for him to cut a cubit or two cubits upward or to carry a ratl or two ratls. And if carrying mountains and ascending to the sky is a sign for the one on whose hand it appears, then it is necessary that the composition of the Quran be a sign for the one who brought it, even if the composition of less than a sura of it is not a sign for anyone.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument (مسألة) about the nature of the Quran's miraculousness (إعجاز). The objector argues that since humans can produce individual words or letters similar to those of the Quran, they can produce the entire Quran, so it is not miraculous. Baqillani refutes this by drawing an analogy: ability to produce a small amount does not entail ability to produce a large amount. For example, being able to utter a word does not make one a poet; being able to drink a sip does not mean one can drink the ocean; being able to lift a small weight does not mean one can carry mountains. The miraculousness of the Quran lies in its entirety, not in its parts. Just as carrying a mountain is a miracle (sign) for a prophet, so the complete composition of the Quran is a miracle for Muhammad. The inability to produce a full sura does not mean that people cannot produce individual words; rather, the challenge is for the whole Quran or a sura of its like, and the failure of the Arabs is evidence of its miraculous nature.

نظم
الآية
القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans can produce words similar to the Quran, they should be able to produce the entire Quran, making it non-miraculous.
Correction: Baqillani shows that ability to produce parts does not imply ability to produce the whole. The whole is qualitatively different and requires a different capacity.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Baqillani use to show that ability to produce parts does not mean ability to produce the whole?
A) The ability to write poetry versus prose.
B) The ability to drink a sip versus the ocean.
C) The ability to speak versus silence.
D) The ability to walk versus run.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miraculousness lies in its whole composition, not in its individual components, so human ability to produce similar parts does not negate its miracle.

The Miracle of the Quran's Arrangement
Baqillani answers an objection about the Quran's miraculousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran's miraculousness lies in its unique arrangement rather than its individual letters.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is not the letters but their divine arrangement.

A common objection against the Quran's miraculousness is that it uses ordinary letters. Baqillani responds by redirecting attention from the raw material to the unique composition. This lesson explores that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening question: How can the Quran be miraculous if it uses the same letters anyone can speak?
Note the objector's assumption: ordinary letters = ordinary speech.
2
Identify Baqillani's distinction
Find where Baqillani states that the miraculousness is not in the letters themselves but in their arrangement and composition.
This distinction is the key to answering the objection.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the doctrine of i'jaz (inimitability). The raw material is common, but the divine ordering is unique.
This shows that the Quran's miracle is structural, not material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في كيفية الإعجاز في القرآن فإن قالوا: كيف يكون القرآن معجزاً وهو غير خارج عن حروف المعجم التي يتكلم بها الخلق من أهل الفصاحة والعي واللكنة؟ قيل لهم: ليس الإعجاز في نفس الحروف وإنما هو في نظمها وإحكام

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (مسألة) about the modality of the Quran's miraculousness. The objector argues that the Quran consists of ordinary Arabic letters that anyone, whether eloquent or not, can pronounce. Therefore, how can it be miraculous? Baqillani gives a concise answer: the miracle is not in the individual letters as such (ذات الحروف), but in their particular arrangement and composition (نظمها وإحكامها). The arrangement is what produces its unique eloquence and inimitability. This is a foundational point in the theology of i'jaz: the raw material is common, but the divine ordering is unique and beyond human capacity. The answer is cut off at the end of the page, but the essential point is stated.

The Miracle of the Quran Cannot Be Imitated Even in Part
Baqillani refutes the objection that partial ability implies full ability to imitate the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ability to produce a few words similar to the Quran does not imply the ability to produce the entire Quran, and how this preserves the Quran's miraculousness.

Core Idea
Ability to imitate a part does not equal ability to imitate the whole.

Baqillani encounters a common objection: if people can produce a word or two like the Quran, why not the whole? In this passage, he dismantles the assumption that partial ability implies full ability. Through vivid analogies, he shows that the Quran's miracle lies in its complete composition, not in its parts. This lesson unpacks his refutation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that if people can produce a single word or letter similar to the Quran, they should be able to produce the entire Quran. Understand this assumption.
2
Follow Baqillani's analogies
He uses examples of poetry, drinking, and lifting to show that inability to do the whole does not imply inability to do a part. Notice how each analogy mirrors the Quranic case.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The miracle is in the complete nazm (composition) of the Quran. The inability to produce a full sura does not negate the miraculousness of the entire Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في صفة الإعجاز في القرآن
فإن قال قائل: فإذا قدر العباد عندكم على مثل الكلمة والكلمتين والحرف والحرفين فما أنكرتم أن يقدروا على مثل جميعه وألا يكون في ذلك إعجاز؟ يقال له: لو وجب ما قلته لوجب إذا قدر الناس على مثل ما سألت عنه أن يكونوا كلهم شعراء وخطباء وأصحاب نظم ورسائل لقدرتهم على الكلمة والاثنتين وهذا جهل ممن صار إليه وليس يجب، إذا تعذر على الإنسان نظم الكثير، أن يتعذر عليه نظم اليسير؛ كما لا يجب إذا عليه شرب ماء البحار والأنهار أن يتعذر عليه شرب الجرعة والجرعتين، وإذا تعذر عليه الصعود إلى السماء وحمل الجبال أن يتعذر عليه قطع الذراع إلى فوق والذراعين وحمل الرطل والرطلين وإذا كان حمل الجبال والصعود إلى السماء آية لمن ظهر على يده وجب أن يكون نظم القرآن آية لمن أتى به وإن لم يكن نظم ما دون سورة منه آية لأحد.
Issue concerning the nature of the miraculousness in the Qur'an. For if someone says: 'If, according to you, the servants are capable of (producing) the like of a word and two words and a letter and two letters, then what prevents you from acknowledging that they are capable of the like of all of it, and that there would be no miraculousness in that?' It is said to him: 'If what you said were necessary, then it would be necessary that if people were capable of the like of what you asked about, they would all be poets and orators and masters of composition and epistles because of their ability with one word and two. This is ignorance on the part of one who adopts it. It is not necessary that if composing much is impossible for a person, that composing little should also be impossible. Just as it is not necessary that if drinking the water of the seas and rivers is impossible for him, that drinking a sip and two sips should be impossible; and if ascending to the sky and carrying mountains is impossible for him, that crossing a cubit or two cubits and carrying a ratl or two ratls should be impossible. And if carrying mountains and ascending to the sky were a miracle for the one on whose hand it appeared, then it is necessary that the composition of the Qur'an be a miracle for the one who brought it, even if the composition of less than a sura from it was not a miracle for anyone.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the nature of the Quran's miraculousness (i'jaz). The objector argues that if people can produce a single word or letter similar to the Quran, then they should be able to produce the entire Quran, negating its miraculousness. The answer refutes this by drawing analogies: the ability to do a small part does not imply the ability to do the whole. For example, a person's inability to compose a long poem does not mean they cannot compose a short one. Similarly, the inability to drink an ocean does not mean one cannot drink a sip. The author uses these examples to show that the inability to produce a full sura does not negate the miraculousness of the entire Quran. The key point is that the miracle lies in the complete composition (nazm) of the Quran as a whole, not in its parts.

The Miracle Lies in Arrangement, Not in the Letters
Baqillani explains that the miracle is in arrangement, not in the letters themselves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran's miraculousness is not in its letters but in their unique arrangement and composition.

Core Idea
The miracle is in the arrangement, not the raw materials.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a common objection: how can the Qur'an be miraculous when it uses the same letters everyone uses? He responds by distinguishing between the letters themselves and their divine arrangement. This clarification is crucial for understanding the nature of the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the skeptics' question: How can the Quran be miraculous if it uses ordinary letters?
2
Find the core response
Notice that Baqillani immediately redirects from the letters to their arrangement (nazm) and perfect ordering (ihkam al-rasf).
3
Understand the analogy
Examine the comparison to the succession of movements. What does that illustrate about arrangement?
4
Reflect on the consequence
If arrangement were not a basis for excellence, then all literary skill would be meaningless. This strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في كيفية الإعجاز في القرآن
فإن قالوا: كيف يكون القرآن معجزاً وهو غير خارج عن حروف المعجم التي يتكلم بها الخلق من أهل الفصاحة والعي واللكنة؟ قيل لهم: ليس الإعجاز في نفس الحروف وإنما هو في نظمها وإحكام رصفها وكونها على وزن ما أتى به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وليس نظمها أكثر من وجودها متقدمة ومتأخرة ومترتبة في الوجود وليس لها نظم سواها وهو كتتابع الحركات إلى السماء ووجود بعضها قبل بعض ووجود بعضها بعد بعض ولو كان ما سألتم عنه يبطل مزية القرآن وموضع الأعجوبة في نظمه لوجب إبطال فضيلة الشاعر المفلق والخطيب المصقع والمرسل الفصيح المقتدر، حتى لا يكون على أحد تكلم باللسان العربي وإن كان أعيا من باقل فضل لسحبان وائل وهذا أيضاً جهل ممن صار إليه: فبطل ما تعلقتم به.
Issue concerning the modality of miraculousness in the Qur'an. For if they said: 'How can the Qur'an be miraculous when it is not outside the letters of the alphabet which people speak, whether eloquent, inarticulate, or having a lisp?' It is said to them: 'The miraculousness is not in the letters themselves, but rather in their arrangement and the perfection of their ordering, and in their being upon the measure of what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought. And their arrangement is nothing more than their existence as preceding and succeeding and ordered in existence, and they have no other arrangement besides that. It is like the succession of movements to the sky, the existence of some before others and the existence of some after others. And if what you asked about invalidated the excellence of the Qur'an and the locus of wonder in its arrangement, then it would be necessary to invalidate the virtue of the exceptional poet, the eloquent orator, and the eloquent accomplished writer, so that no one who spoke in the Arabic tongue would have any excellence, even if he were more inarticulate than Baqil in comparison with Sahban Wail. This also is ignorance on the part of one who adopts it. So what you have held to is invalid.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the modality (kayfiyya) of the Quran's miraculousness. The objectors ask how the Quran can be miraculous when it uses the same letters that all people use. The answer clarifies that the miracle is not in the letters themselves (al-huruf) but in their arrangement (nazm) and perfect composition (ihkam rasfiha). The author argues that the arrangement is simply the order of letters—some preceding, some succeeding—and that this order is like the succession of movements. If this arrangement were not miraculous, then all literary excellence would be undermined, from the most eloquent poet to the most inarticulate speaker. The point is that the Quran's unique and inimitable arrangement is what constitutes its miracle, not the raw materials.

Key Terms
إعجاز
حروف المعجم
نظم
رصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle must involve different substances or materials.
Correction: The Quran's miracle lies in the unique arrangement of ordinary letters, not in the letters themselves.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, where does the miraculousness of the Quran reside?
A) In the individual letters
B) In the arrangement and composition
C) In its length
D) In its poetic meter

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miracle is in its perfect arrangement, not in the letters that compose it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your worship or deeds are ordinary because they look the same as everyone else's? How does Baqillani's point that value lies in arrangement—not the components—challenge that feeling? Will you commit today to seeking excellence in the 'how' of your actions, not just the 'what'?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you pause to consider the precise order of its words? If the miracle is in the arrangement, how should that change the care with which you recite and reflect? What one step will you take this week to slow down and meditate on the structure of a single verse?
?
The objection in the lesson assumes that if something is common, it cannot be special. Where in your life have you dismissed something because it seems ordinary? What would it mean to recognize that the ordinary can become extraordinary through perfect ordering—and how might you apply this to your daily routine?

The Challenge Is with the Arranged Letters
Baqillani answers an objection about the challenge with the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's distinction between the eternal speech of God and the arranged letters of the Quran, and why the challenge (tahaddi) is directed at the latter.

Core Idea
The Quranic challenge targets the arranged letters, not the eternal attribute of speech.

This lesson addresses a common objection to the Islamic doctrine of the Quran's eternity. Critics ask how the Quran can be a miracle if it is eternal and unlike human speech. Baqillani clarifies that the challenge is with the expressed letters, which are of the same kind as human language, making the miracle valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector (from Jews, Christians, Mu'tazila) questions how the Quran can be challenged if it is eternal and unlike human speech.
2
Identify Baqillani's answer
Notice that Baqillani immediately distinguishes between the eternal speech (kalam qaim bi-llah) and the arranged letters (huruf manzuma). The Prophet challenged with the letters, not the eternal attribute.
The answer is cut off, but the key distinction is clear.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the Quran's miracle while maintaining that God's eternal speech is beyond imitation.
This distinction was important in debates with rationalists and other faiths.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التحدي بالحروف المنظومة لا بالكلام القائم بالله
فإن قال قائل من اليهود والنصارى والمعتزلة: كيف يجوز التحدي بمثل القرآن وهو عندكم قديم لا مثل له من كلام الآدميين ولا يجانِسُ كلام المخلوقين؟ قيل له: لم يَتَحَدَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بمثل الكلام القائم بالله سبحانه وإنما تحداهم بمثل الحروف المنظومة التي هي
Issue concerning the challenge with the arranged letters, not with the speech subsisting in God. It is said to him: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) did not issue a challenge with the like of the speech subsisting in God, may He be exalted; rather, he challenged them with the like of the arranged letters which are...'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer concerning the legitimacy of the challenge (tahaddi) with the Quran. The objectors—Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila—argue that since the Quran is eternal (qadim) and incomparable to human speech, it cannot be used as a challenge. The answer distinguishes between the eternal speech of God (al-kalam al-qa'im bi-llah), which is an attribute of God, and the expressed letters (al-huruf al-manzuma) that constitute the recited Quran. The Prophet's challenge was directed at the arranged letters, not at the eternal attribute. The answer is cut off, but the key point is that the letters are of the same kind as human speech and thus a valid basis for challenge.

The Quran's Arrangement as Its Ordered Existence
Baqillani refutes the claim that the Quran's arrangement is a separate property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the Quran's composition is not separate from its existence, but identical to its ordered existence, and how this preserves its miraculous nature.

Core Idea
The Quran's arrangement is its ordered existence, not an extra layer.

Imam Baqillani addresses a key objection: if the Quran's letters exist in a specific order, that order might seem like an accidental property. He argues, however, that the very order in which the letters exist is its composition—there is no separate 'arrangement' over and above the letters themselves. This refutation is crucial because it upholds the Quran's inimitability against those who would treat its arrangement as trivial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's own rhythm and argument come through.
Notice the contrast between what the objector claims and what Baqillani asserts.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence where Baqillani says that the composition (nazm) is not more than the existence of the letters as ordered, preceding and succeeding.
This is the key idea: the arrangement is intrinsic to existence.
3
Understand the reductio argument
Baqillani shows that if the objection were valid, it would destroy all human eloquence. Follow his logic: from the Quran to poets and orators.
This is a common strategy in kalam: showing the absurd consequences of an opponent's view.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this argument matter for the Quran's miraculous nature? Think about what it preserves.
If arrangement were separate, it could be accidental; but here it is essential, making the inimitability inherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رَصْفِهَا وَكَوْنُهَا عَلَى وَزْنِ مَا أَتَى بِهِ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَلَيْسَ نَظْمُهَا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ وُجُودِهَا مُتَقَدِّمَةً وَمُتَأَخِّرَةً وَمُتَرَتِّبَةً فِي الْوُجُودِ وَلَيْسَ لَهَا نَظْمٌ سِوَاهَا وَهُوَ كَتَتَابُعِ الْحَرَكَاتِ إِلَى السَّمَاءِ وَوُجُودِ بَعْضِهَا قَبْلَ بَعْضٍ وَوُجُودِ بَعْضِهَا بَعْدَ بَعْضٍ وَلَوْ كَانَ مَا سَأَلْتُمْ عَنْهُ يُبْطِلُ مَزِيَّةَ الْقُرْآنِ وَمَوْضِعَ الْأَعْجُوبَةِ فِي نَظْمِهِ لَوَجَبَ إِبْطَالُ فَضِيلَةِ الشَّاعِرِ الْمُفْلِقِ وَالْخَطِيبِ الْمِصْقَعِ وَالْمُرْسَلِ الْفَصِيحِ الْمُقْتَدِرِ، حَتَّى لَا يَكُونَ عَلَى أَحَدٍ تَكَلَّمَ بِاللِّسَانِ الْعَرَبِيِّ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَعْيَا مِنْ بَاقِلٍ فَضْلًا لِسَحْبَانَ وَائِلٍ وَهَذَا أَيْضًا جَهْلٌ مِمَّنْ صَارَ إِلَيْهِ : فَبَطَلَ مَا تَعَلَّقْتُمْ بِهِ.
Its arrangement, and that it is of the scale of what the Prophet brought, and its composition is not more than its existence as preceding, succeeding, and ordered in existence, and it has no composition other than it, and it is like the sequence of movements to the sky, and the existence of some before some and the existence of some after some. And if what you asked about invalidates the merit of the Quran and the locus of the wonder in its composition, then it would be necessary to invalidate the excellence of the brilliant poet, the eloquent orator, and the eloquent skilled messenger, so that no one who spoke the Arabic tongue would have excellence, even if he were more tongue-tied than Baqil, to say nothing of Suhban Wa'il. And this is ignorance from whoever adopts it. So what you adhered to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the arrangement (rasf) of the Quran. The author Baqillani begins by stating that the arrangement of the Quran's letters is not something additional to their existence; rather, the ordered existence of the letters themselves constitutes its composition. He compares it to the sequential movements toward the sky, where the order is intrinsic. He then argues that if the objector's claim (that such ordering is not miraculous) were true, it would also invalidate all human eloquence—from the most brilliant poet to the most skilled orator. This would mean no Arabic speaker has any excellence, even the famously tongue-tied Baqil or the eloquent Suhban Wa'il. This is absurd, so the objection is due to ignorance and thus invalid.

Correction: Baqillani argues that the arrangement is identical to the ordered existence of the letters themselves; there is no separate 'composition' over and above their sequential existence.

The Challenge is with Composed Letters, Not the Eternal Speech
Baqillani answers the objection that the Quran cannot be challenged because it is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic challenge (tahaddi) pertains to the composed letters, not the eternal speech, and how this refutes the objection that the Quran cannot be imitated.

Core Idea
The Quranic challenge targets the humanly attainable expression, not the uncreated attribute.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses a critical objection raised by Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila: How can the Quran be challengingly inimitable if it is eternal and has no equivalent in human speech? He clarifies that the challenge is not directed at the eternal speech itself, but at the composed letters that express it. This distinction answers the objection and preserves the miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection from the Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila. Understand why they think the challenge is impossible.
2
Understand the answer
Notice how Baqillani distinguishes between the eternal speech (kalam qadim) and the composed letters (huruf manzuma). The challenge is about the latter.
The composed letters are the revealed text we recite.
3
Realize the implication
See how this refutes the objection: since the challenge concerns humanly achievable eloquence, it is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَسْأَلَةٌ فِي التَّحَدِّي بِالْحُرُوفِ الْمَنْظُومَةِ لَا بِالْكَلَامِ الْقَائِمِ بِاللَّهِ. فَإِنْ قَالَ قَائِلٌ مِنَ الْيَهُودِ وَالنَّصَارَى وَالْمُعْتَزِلَةِ : كَيْفَ يَجُوزُ التَّحَدِّي بِمِثْلِ الْقُرْآنِ وَهُوَ عِنْدَكُمْ قَدِيمٌ لَا مِثْلَ لَهُ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْآدَمِيِّينَ وَلَا يُجَانِسُ كَلَامَ الْمَخْلُوقِينَ؟ قِيلَ لَهُ : لَمْ يَتَحَدَّ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ بِمِثْلِ الْكَلَامِ الْقَائِمِ بِاللَّهِ سُبْحَانَهُ وَإِنَّمَا تَحَدَّاهُمْ بِمِثْلِ الْحُرُوفِ الْمَنْظُومَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْهُ فِي بَرَاعَتِهَا وَفَصَاحَتِهَا وَاخْتِصَارِهَا وَكَثْرَةِ مَعَانِيهَا وَإِذَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ كَذَلِكَ بَطَلَ مَا سَأَلْتُمْ عَنْهُ.
Issue concerning the challenge by the composed letters, not by the speech subsisting in Allah. If a questioner from the Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila says: 'How is the challenge by something like the Quran permissible when according to you it is eternal, having no like from the speech of humans, and it is not homogenous with the speech of creatures?' It is said to him: 'The Prophet did not challenge by something like the speech subsisting in Allah, Exalted is He. Rather, he challenged them by something like the composed letters that are an expression of it, in their brilliance, eloquence, conciseness, and abundance of meanings. And if that is the case, what you asked about becomes invalid.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Baqillani first states the issue: the tahaddi (challenge) concerns the composed letters (al-huruf al-manzumah), not the eternal speech (al-kalam al-qa'im bi-llah). The objection comes from Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila: how can one challenge with something like the Quran if it is eternal and has no equivalent in human speech? The answer clarifies that the Prophet's challenge was not to produce the eternal speech itself, but to produce something like the composed letters that express that speech. These letters are characterized by brilliance, eloquence, conciseness, and richness of meaning. Since the challenge pertains to the humanly achievable expression (in terms of eloquence), not the uncreated attribute, the objection fails.

حروف منظومة
كلام قديم
اعجاز

Misconception: That the Quranic challenge (tahaddi) was directed at producing the eternal speech itself.
Correction: It was actually directed at the composed letters (the revealed text) that express the eternal speech.

Challenging with the Eternal Speech
Baqillani adds that challenging with the eternal speech is valid under certain conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition under which Allah and His Messenger may challenge using the eternal speech, and how this refutes the objection against the Qur'an's inimitability.

Core Idea
The eternal speech can be used in challenge when someone claims it is like human speech.

Imam Baqillani continues his discussion on the Qur'an's miraculous nature by addressing a specific objection: what if someone denies that the challenge (tahaddi) applies to the eternal speech? Here, he clarifies that the challenge can indeed be issued with the eternal speech, but only under a particular condition—when an atheist or obstinate person claims that the eternal speech is similar to human speech. This lesson unpacks that condition and the supporting evidence from the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Baqillani's argument. Notice the condition he sets: the challenge is valid only when someone claims the eternal speech is like human speech.
The passage includes three Quranic verses as evidence.
2
Identify the condition and the method
Find the condition (when an atheist or obstinate person claims equality with human speech) and the method of challenge (demanding they produce something like it).
3
See the Quranic support
Look at the three verses cited: they are examples of Allah challenging disbelievers in various contexts. Reflect on how they parallel the challenge with eternal speech.
The verses show that Allah demands proof, plot, or partners from those who make false claims.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that if the condition holds, the wonder of the objectors falls away and no one has a valid ground to reject the inimitability of the Quran.
This reinforces that the eternal speech's inimitability remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَصِحُّ مِنَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَمِنْ رَسُولِهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَنْ يَتَحَدَّى بِمِثْلِ الْكَلَامِ الْقَدِيمِ إِذَا ادَّعَى مُلْحِدٌ أَوْ مُعَانِدٌ أَنَّهُ مِثْلُ كَلَامِ الْآدَمِيِّينَ وَمِنْ جِنْسِ كَلَامِ الْمَخْلُوقِينَ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ لَهُ : ائْتِ بِمِثْلِهِ إِنْ كَانَ لَهُ مِثْلٌ عَلَى مَا تَدَّعِيهِ، كَمَا قَالَ تَعَالَى (قُلْ هَاتُوا بُرْهَانَكُمْ إِنْ كُنْتُمْ صَادِقِينَ) وَ (فَإِنْ كَانَ لَكُمْ كَيْدٌ فَكِيدُونِ) وَ (أَيْنَ شُرَكَاؤُكُمُ الَّذِينَ كُنْتُمْ تَزْعُمُونَ)؛ فَطَالَبَهُمْ بِذَلِكَ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ التَّحَدِّي وَالتَّقْرِيعِ إِنْ كَانَ لِلْبَاطِلِ بُرْهَانٌ وَلِلْقَدِيمِ سُبْحَانَهُ شَرِيكٌ أَوْ كَيْدٌ يُكَادُ بِهِ وَإِذَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ كَذَلِكَ سَقَطَ تَعَجُّبُهُمْ مِنْ ذَلِكَ وَثَبَتَ أَنَّهُ لَا تَعَلُّقَ لِأَحَدٍ فِيهِ.
Moreover, it is valid for Allah Almighty and His Messenger to challenge by something like the eternal speech if an atheist or an obstinate person claims that it is like the speech of humans and of the same kind as the speech of creatures, by saying to him: 'Bring something like it if it has a likeness according to your claim' – just as Allah said: 'Say: Bring your proof if you are truthful' (Quran 2:111), and 'Then if you have a plot, plot against Me' (Quran 77:39), and 'Where are your partners whom you used to claim?' (Quran 6:22). So He demanded them on the basis of challenge and rebuke, if falsehood had a proof and the Eternal, Exalted is He, had a partner or a plot by which He is plotted. And if that is the case, their wonder at that falls away, and it is established that there is no ground for anyone in it.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage provides an additional argument to the previous lesson. Baqillani states that it is also permissible for Allah and His Messenger to challenge with the eternal speech itself, but only under a specific condition: when an atheist (mulhid) or obstinate person (mu'anid) claims that the eternal speech is like human speech. In that case, the response is to demand that they produce its like, based on their own claim. Baqillani supports this with Quranic verses where Allah challenges the disbelievers to bring proof, plot, or partners. This is a method of challenge and rebuke (tahaddi wa taqri'). The conclusion: if such a claim is false, the challenger's astonishment disappears, and it is established that no one has any ground (i.e., no valid objection) against the Quran's inimitability.

Key Terms
تحدي
ملحد
معاند
كلام قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Quranic challenge (tahaddi) only concerns the composed, revealed words (the Qur'an as we have it) and never the eternal speech (kalam qadim). They may object that challenging with the eternal speech is invalid because it is not composed of letters and sounds.
Correction: This passage shows that the challenge can indeed involve the eternal speech when someone claims that the eternal speech is similar to human speech. In that case, the challenge is legitimate because it is based on the opponent's own assertion. The eternal speech's inimitability is defended by demanding that the opponent produce its like, which they cannot.

Checkpoint

A) When someone denies the Prophethood of Muhammad.
B) When an atheist or obstinate person claims the eternal speech is like human speech.
C) When someone asks for a miracle of the Prophet.
D) When the challenge is directed at Jews and Christians.

Key Takeaway
The challenge with the eternal speech is valid only when someone claims it is like human speech, and this refutes any ground for rejecting the Qur'an's inimitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Quran's Inimitability and the Divine Challenge
Baqillani argues for Quranic inimitability via its qualities and the challenge to disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument for the Quran's inimitability based on its unique qualities and the divine challenge (tahaddi), and how this refutes the disbelievers' objections.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is proven by the failed challenge to produce anything like it.

This lesson explores a key proof for the Quran's miraculous nature (i'jaz). Imam Baqillani presents a concise argument: the Quran's unmatched linguistic qualities, combined with the divine challenge to disbelievers to produce something similar, establishes its divine origin. The passage also addresses the logical fallacy of equating the Quran with human speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Quran's qualities
Notice the four qualities: brilliance (barā'ah), eloquence (faṣāḥah), conciseness (ikhtiṣār), and abundance of meanings (kathrat ma'ānī). Ask: why are these relevant to inimitability?
These qualities collectively make the Quran surpass any human speech.
2
Grasp the logic of the challenge
Baqillani argues that if someone claims the Quran is like human speech, they are required to produce a similar example. This challenge is based on the impossibility of doing so.
The challenge is not just theoretical; it was issued historically.
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Baqillani quotes three verses that command disbelievers to bring proof, plot, or show their partners. These verses support the challenge and reproach.
Notice the progression from ordinary proof to plot to partners.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that if falsehood had any proof or if Allah had a partner or plot, the disbelievers' wonder would be warranted. But since they cannot produce anything, their wonder is invalid.
The conclusion establishes that no one can match the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارة عنه في براعتها وفصاحتها واختصارها وكثرة معانيها وإذا كان ذلك كذلك بَطَلَ ما سألتم عنه . على أنه يصح من الله تعالى صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يتحدّى بمثل الكلام القديم إذا ادعى ملحد أو معاند أنه مثل كلام الآدميين ومن جنس كلام المخلوقين بأن يقال له : إيت بمثله إن كان له مثل على ما تدعيه، كما قال تعالى : قل هاتوا برهانكم إن كنتم صادقين ، وقال : فإن كان لكم كيد فكيدون ، وقال : أين شركاؤكم الذين كنتم تزعمون ؛ فطالبهم بذلك على مذهب التحدي والتقريع أني إن كان للباطل برهان وللقديم سبحانه شريك أو كيد يكاد به وإذا كان ذلك كذلك سقط تعجبهم من ذلك وثبت أنه لا تعلق لأحد فيه .
An expression of it in its brilliance, eloquence, conciseness, and abundance of meanings. And when that is the case, what you asked about becomes invalid. Indeed, it is valid for Allah—may Allah bless him and grant him peace—to challenge with the like of the ancient speech if a heretic or obstinate claims that it is like human speech and of the genus of created speech, by saying to him: 'Bring something like it if it has a likeness according to what you claim,' as Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: Bring your proof if you are truthful,' and 'If you have a plot, then plot against Me,' and 'Where are your partners whom you claimed?' So He demands that from them in the manner of challenge and reproof, that if falsehood has a proof or the Eternal—glorified be He—has a partner or a plot by which it is plotted. And when that is the case, their wonderment at that falls away, and it is established that no one has any claim in it.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the inimitability of the Quran. Baqillani first describes the Quran's qualities—brilliance, eloquence, conciseness, and abundance of meanings. He then argues that if these qualities are present, the disbelievers' objection is invalid. It is valid for Allah to challenge anyone who claims the Quran resembles human speech to produce something like it. He supports this by quoting three Qur'anic verses that command disbelievers to bring proof, plot if they can, and identify their claimed partners. This challenge is in the mode of tahaddi (defiance) and taqri' (reproach). The passage concludes that if falsehood had any proof or if Allah had a partner or counter-plot, the disbelievers' wonder would be justified, but since it is not, their wonder ceases and it is established that no one can match the Quran.

Misconception: Some may think the Quran's inimitability is merely subjective or based on personal opinion.
Correction: Baqillani grounds it in an objective historical challenge: the Arabs were unable to produce similar speech despite their motivation and ability, which is a verifiable fact.

Checkpoint

B) The Quran's brilliance, eloquence, conciseness, and abundance of meanings

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you read the Quran, do you feel the weight of its demand for proof? Or do you read it passively, as if its words were no different from any other book? How would your reading change if you truly believed every verse is a direct challenge to your soul?
?
Baqillani says the disbelievers' wonderment falls away when the proof is established. What wonderment in your own heart—doubts, excuses, or indifference—must fall away for you to submit completely to the Quran's authority? Will you allow it?

Refuting the Memorization Objection
Baqillani refutes the claim that a memorizer can use the Quran as his own miracle sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection that a person who memorizes the Quran could claim it as his own miracle sign.

Core Idea
Memorization does not transfer authorship. The Quran remains inimitable.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses a clever objection: if the Quran is a miracle, couldn't someone simply memorize it and claim it as his own sign? This would seem to undermine its uniqueness. In this passage, he gives two decisive reasons why such a claim fails. Both reasons protect the Quran's status as an inimitable miracle while also demonstrating the depth of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's claim: if the Quran is miraculous, a memorizer reciting it to his town should have it accepted as his miracle sign. Notice the logical structure.
The objector assumes memorization equals authorship.
2
Examine the first response
Baqillani argues that a hearer must verify that the text originated from the claimant. Minimal research reveals it is already known from the Prophet, nullifying the claim.
The miracle status depends on context and verification.
3
Examine the second response
Allah could intervene by making the memorizer forget or by enabling the listener to memorize it as well, exposing the claim.
Divine power can protect the miracle's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : لو كان القرآن معجزاً لوجب إذا حفظه بعض الناس وتلاه على أهل بلده وادعاه آية له أن يكون دلالة على صدقه. قيل له : لا يجب ما قلته من وجهين: أحدهما أن القرآن لا يكون معجزاً عند من سمعه إلا بعد فحصه وبحثه وتفتيشه وعلمه بأنه لا أحد سبق الذي أتى به إليه، وأنه من جهته نجم ومن قبله ظهر. ومتى لم يعلم ذلك لم يعلمه معجزاً له. وحافظ القرآن إذا ادعاه آية لم يلبث سامع دعواه مع أدنى بحث حتى يعلم أنه ظاهر لغيره فسقط احتجاجه به. والوجه الآخر أن الله تعالى إذا علم ذلك إياه وذهب بحفظه من قلبه أو خلق لسامعه القدرة على حفظه من أوله إلى آخره حتى يقول لمن أتى به : هذا أمر حفظناه وعرفناه ولعلك عني أخذته. فسقط التعلق بهذا.
If someone says: If the Quran were miraculous, then if some people memorize it and recite it to the people of his town and claim it as his sign, it would have to be evidence of his truthfulness. He is told: What you said is not necessary, for two reasons: First, the Quran is not considered miraculous by the one who hears it except after examining, researching, and investigating it, and knowing that no one preceded the one who brought it to him, and that it originated from him and appeared before him. And when he does not know that, he does not know it as miraculous for him. And the memorizer of the Quran, if he claims it as his sign, the hearer of his claim will not delay with minimal research until he knows that it is apparent from someone else, so his argument with it falls. Second, Allah the Exalted, if He knows that of him, could take away its memorization from his heart or create for the listener the ability to memorize it from beginning to end, so that he says to the one who brought it: 'This is something we have memorized and known, and perhaps you took it from me.' So the attachment to this falls.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues: if the Quran is miraculous, then a person who memorizes it and claims it as his own sign should have that claim accepted. Baqillani gives two answers. First, the Quran's status as a miracle depends on verification: hearers know it is not from the claimant because they recognize it from prior revelation. Second, Allah could intervene by making the memorizer forget or by enabling the listener to memorize it too, thus exposing the claim as false. Both answers show that the objection does not undermine the Quran's inimitability.

Key Terms
إعجاز
حفظ
آية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Memorizing the Quran makes it one's own personal miracle sign.
Correction: Baqillani shows that memorization does not confer originality; the hearer can verify the source and Allah can intervene to expose the fraud.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Baqillani gives against the claim that memorizing the Quran could serve as a personal miracle sign?
A) Memorizers are not truthful
B) The hearer would know it is already known from someone else after minimal research
C) The Quran cannot be memorized completely
D) Only prophets can perform miracles

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is not undermined by human memorization because originality is verifiable and divine power can expose fraud.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or listen to the Quran, do you take its miraculous nature for granted, or do you actively reflect on its unique origin? What would it take for you to truly investigate and verify its inimitability as Baqillani suggests?
?
Have you ever presented someone else's work or ideas as your own? How does that relate to the memorizer's flawed claim, and what does that reveal about your own need for authenticity before Allah?
?
If Allah were to test you by making you forget a portion of the Quran you had memorized, how would you react? Would your reliance be on your memory or on Allah's mercy and the Quran's eternal truth?

Why the Torah and Gospel Are Not Miraculous
Baqillani explains why the Torah and Gospel lack the cause for inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) depends on a historical context of challenge and failure, and why the Torah and Gospel do not meet that criterion.

Core Idea
A scripture's miracle requires a public challenge met with failure.

Baqillani addresses a common question: why aren't the Torah and Gospel considered miracles like the Quran? He explains that the Quran's inimitability is not an abstract property but is tied to a concrete historical event: the Arabs' inability to produce anything like it despite their strong desire to refute it. This passage clarifies that the cause (illa) of the Quran's miracle is its context of challenge and failure, which the Torah and Gospel lack.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question begins with 'If they say...' and the answer starts with 'He is told...'.
The passage is a dialogue: an objection and a response.
2
Identify the cause of the Quran's miracle
Find the phrase 'the cause by which the Quran was miraculous' and note the description: the Arabs' inability to oppose it despite their eagerness and hostility.
This cause is central to Baqillani's argument.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Baqillani presents a conditional: if Moses and Jesus had challenged their enemies and the enemies failed, then their scriptures would be miraculous. Since that did not happen, the Torah and Gospel are not miraculous.
This shows that the miracle is tied to a specific historical scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: ما أنكرتم أن تكون التوراة والإنجيل معجزاً؟ قيل لهم: أنكرنا ذلك لعدم العلة التي لها كان القرآن معجزاً وهي عجز العرب عن معارضة مورده مع حرصهم على تكذيبه وما عره وغض منه وإيثارهم لقتله وبلوغ كل غاية في مكارِهه وفض الجمع من حوله. فلو تحدى موسى وعيسى عليهما السلام أعداءهما بمثل التوراة والإنجيل وغيرهم من أهل الأهواء والملحدين فعجزوا عند التحدي عن ذلك لوجب أن يكون ما أتيا به من ذلك معجزاً. وإذا لم يكن ذلك كذلك لم يجب ما قلتموه.
If they say: What prevents you from considering the Torah and the Gospel as miraculous? He is told: We deny that due to the absence of the cause by which the Quran was miraculous, which is the inability of the Arabs to oppose its bringer despite their eagerness to belie him and what disgraced him and contempt for him and their preference for killing him and reaching every extreme in his hardships and the disbanding of the assembly around him. So if Moses and Jesus, peace be upon them, had challenged their enemies with the like of the Torah and the Gospel, and likewise the people of desires and heretics, and they were incapable at the challenge of that, then it would have been necessary that what they brought from that would be miraculous. And if that is not the case, then what you said is not necessary.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer regarding the miraculous nature of the Torah and Gospel. The questioner asks why these scriptures are not considered miraculous. Baqillani answers that the cause (illa) for the Quran's inimitability is the specific historical context: the Arabs were desperate to disprove the Quran but unable to produce anything like it. The Torah and Gospel were not challenged in such a context, so they lack that cause. Had Moses and Jesus challenged their enemies and been met with inability, their scriptures would have been miraculous. Since that did not happen, the claim that they are miraculous is unfounded.

Key Terms
إعجاز
علة
توراة
إنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any holy book is automatically a miracle because it comes from God.
Correction: Baqillani shows that a miracle requires a historical challenge and the inability to meet it; the Torah and Gospel were not challenged in that context.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the specific cause (illa) that makes the Quran miraculous?
A) It is the word of God
B) The Arabs' inability to oppose it despite their intense efforts to disprove it
C) It contains prophecies
D) It was revealed to the best prophet

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is tied to a specific historical challenge that the Arabs failed to meet; the Torah and Gospel lack this context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been taught that the Quran is miraculous. But can you defend its uniqueness using Baqillani's specific argument about the public challenge, or do you rely on general statements? If you cannot, what does that reveal about the depth of your understanding of your own faith?
?
Baqillani shows that the Arabs were desperate to disprove the Quran—they hated the Prophet and tried everything to refute him. How desperate are you to verify the truth of your beliefs? Have you ever sincerely tested the Quran's claim to be from God, or do you avoid the challenge for fear of what you might find?
?
The Torah and Gospel are not considered miraculous because they lacked a public challenge. Reflect on your own life: what miracles have you personally witnessed or experienced? Have you ever truly challenged God to show you a sign, or do you take your faith for granted? What would it take for you to be as certain as the Arabs were of the Quran's inimitability?

Why the Torah and Gospel Are Not Miracles Like the Quran
Baqillani refutes the claim that Torah and Gospel are miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a scripture is considered a miracle only if its prophet issued a specific challenge to contemporaries who were experts in the relevant field and were unable to meet it.

Core Idea
A miracle requires a real challenge and demonstrated incapacity.

This passage addresses an objection: why not consider the Torah and Gospel miracles like the Quran? Baqillani explains that the Qur'an's miraculous nature stems from the Arabs' inability to match it despite their fierce opposition. For the Torah and Gospel to be miracles, their prophets would have had to issue similar challenges to experts in the arts of their time, which did not happen. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opening line where the interlocutor asks why the Torah and Gospel cannot be considered miracles. Then read the translation.
Notice the phrasing 'ما أنكرتم' — it’s a rhetorical challenge.
2
Identify the cause of the Quran's miracle
Baqillani states that the Quran is a miracle because of a specific cause: the Arabs, despite their eagerness to discredit the Prophet, were unable to oppose him. Find the Arabic words for this cause.
Key term: 'علة' (cause).
3
Understand the condition for miracle status
Baqillani then spells out the condition: if Moses and Jesus had challenged their enemies (sorcerers, physicians) and they failed, their scriptures would be miracles. Since no such challenge is recorded, the claim fails.
The condition is explicit: challenge + inability.
4
Summarize the argument
In your own words, state why the Torah and Gospel are not miracles according to Baqillani. Then check your understanding with the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If they say: What prevents you from considering the Torah and the Gospel as miracles? It is said to them: We deny that because of the absence of the cause for which the Quran was miraculous, which is the inability of the Arabs to oppose its bringer, with their eagerness to deny him, and whatever disgraced him and belittled him, and their preference for killing him, and reaching every extreme in its hardships, and the scattering of the crowd around him. Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the Torah and Gospel are miracles like the Quran. The author states that the Quran's miracle is established by a specific cause: the Arabs, despite their intense desire to discredit the Prophet (due to enmity, jealousy, and willingness to endure hardships) were unable to produce anything like it. For the Torah and Gospel to be considered miracles, their prophets would have had to issue a similar challenge to contemporaries who were experts in the relevant fields (e.g., sorcerers for Moses, physicians for Jesus) and demonstrated incapacity. Since no such challenge is recorded, the claim is unfounded.

?
